Anion-π interactions occur when an anion is stabilized by the π-system of an electron-deficient aromatic ring. The most prominent classes of such hosts include:\n\n1. Electron-deficient aromatic macrocycles: These include cyclophanes, calixarenes, and resorcinarenes that have been functionalized with electron-withdrawing groups (e.g., nitro, cyano, or perfluoroaryl substituents) to create π-acidic surfaces. These macrocycles can encapsulate anions within their cavities, stabilizing them via anion-π interactions.\n\n2. Naphthalenediimide (NDI) and perylenediimide (PDI) derivatives: These planar aromatic systems, when substituted with electron-withdrawing groups, serve as strong π-acidic hosts for anions. They are often incorporated into larger host frameworks or used as building blocks for anion receptors.\n\n3. Triazine-based hosts: Aromatic triazine rings are inherently electron-deficient and can be used as platforms for anion-π binding, especially when incorporated into macrocyclic or cage-like structures.\n\n4. Fluorinated aromatic hosts: Perfluorinated benzene or naphthalene derivatives are highly π-acidic and have been shown to bind anions via anion-π interactions, either as simple hosts or as part of larger supramolecular assemblies.\n\n5. Foldamers and molecular tweezers: Some foldamers and tweezer-type molecules are designed with electron-deficient aromatic arms that converge to create a π-acidic pocket for anion binding.\n\nIn all these cases, the key design principle is the incorporation of electron-deficient aromatic surfaces to maximize the strength and selectivity of the anion-π interaction. These hosts are distinct from those that rely primarily on hydrogen bonding or electrostatic interactions for anion recognition.\n\nKey literature examples include:\n- Sessler, J. L.; Gale, P. A.; Cho, W.-S. 'Anion Receptor Chemistry', Royal Society of Chemistry, 2006.\n- Ballester, P. 'Anion-π Interactions in Supramolecular Chemistry', Chem. Soc. Anion-π interactions occur when an anion is stabilized by the π-system of an electron-deficient aromatic ring. The most prominent classes of such hosts include:\n\n1. Electron-deficient aromatic macrocycles: These include cyclophanes, calixarenes, and resorcinarenes that have been functionalized with electron-withdrawing groups (e.g., nitro, cyano, or perfluoroaryl substituents) to create π-acidic surfaces. These macrocycles can encapsulate anions within their cavities, stabilizing them via anion-π interactions.\n\n2. Naphthalenediimide (NDI) and perylenediimide (PDI) derivatives: These planar aromatic systems, when substituted with electron-withdrawing groups, serve as strong π-acidic hosts for anions. They are often incorporated into larger host frameworks or used as building blocks for anion receptors.\n\n3. Triazine-based hosts: Aromatic triazine rings are inherently electron-deficient and can be used as platforms for anion-π binding, especially when incorporated into macrocyclic or cage-like structures.\n\n4. Fluorinated aromatic hosts: Perfluorinated benzene or naphthalene derivatives are highly π-acidic and have been shown to bind anions via anion-π interactions, either as simple hosts or as part of larger supramolecular assemblies.\n\n5. Foldamers and molecular tweezers: Some foldamers and tweezer-type molecules are designed with electron-deficient aromatic arms that converge to create a π-acidic pocket for anion binding.\n\nIn all these cases, the key design principle is the incorporation of electron-deficient aromatic surfaces to maximize the strength and selectivity of the anion-π interaction. These hosts are distinct from those that rely primarily on hydrogen bonding or electrostatic interactions for anion recognition.\n\nKey literature examples include:\n- Sessler, J. L.; Gale, P. A.; Cho, W.-S. 'Anion Receptor Chemistry', Royal Society of Chemistry, 2006.\n- Ballester, P. 'Anion-π Interactions in Supramolecular Chemistry', Chem. Soc. The tool analysis would prioritize classes such as 'supramolecular host', 'anion receptor', and 'anion-π interaction', and would look for subclasses or examples with high information richness related to these concepts.", 'hypothetical_answer': "Several types of supramolecular hosts are known to bind anions primarily through anion-π interactions. Anion-π interactions occur when an anion is stabilized by the π-system of an electron-deficient aromatic ring. The most prominent classes of such hosts include:\n\n1. Electron-deficient aromatic macrocycles: These include cyclophanes, calixarenes, and resorcinarenes that have been functionalized with electron-withdrawing groups (e.g., nitro, cyano, or perfluoroaryl substituents) to create π-acidic surfaces. These macrocycles can encapsulate anions within their cavities, stabilizing them via anion-π interactions.\n\n2. Naphthalenediimide (NDI) and perylenediimide (PDI) derivatives: These planar aromatic systems, when substituted with electron-withdrawing groups, serve as strong π-acidic hosts for anions. They are often incorporated into larger host frameworks or used as building blocks for anion receptors.\n\n3. Triazine-based hosts: Aromatic triazine rings are inherently electron-deficient and can be used as platforms for anion-π binding, especially when incorporated into macrocyclic or cage-like structures.\n\n4. Fluorinated aromatic hosts: Perfluorinated benzene or naphthalene derivatives are highly π-acidic and have been shown to bind anions via anion-π interactions, either as simple hosts or as part of larger supramolecular assemblies.\n\n5. Foldamers and molecular tweezers: Some foldamers and tweezer-type molecules are designed with electron-deficient aromatic arms that converge to create a π-acidic pocket for anion binding.\n\nIn all these cases, the key design principle is the incorporation of electron-deficient aromatic surfaces to maximize the strength and selectivity of the anion-π interaction. These hosts are distinct from those that rely primarily on hydrogen bonding or electrostatic interactions for anion recognition.\n\nKey literature examples include:\n- Sessler, J. L.; Gale, P. A.; Cho, W.-S. 'Anion Receptor Chemistry', Royal Society of Chemistry, 2006.\n- Ballester, P. 'Anion-π Interactions in Supramolecular Chemistry', Chem. Soc. Anion-π interactions occur when an anion is stabilized by the π-system of an electron-deficient aromatic ring. The most prominent classes of such hosts include:\n\n1. Electron-deficient aromatic macrocycles: These include cyclophanes, calixarenes, and resorcinarenes that have been functionalized with electron-withdrawing groups (e.g., nitro, cyano, or perfluoroaryl substituents) to create π-acidic surfaces. Naphthalenediimide (NDI) and perylenediimide (PDI) derivatives: These planar aromatic systems, when substituted with electron-withdrawing groups, serve as strong π-acidic hosts for anions. Triazine-based hosts: Aromatic triazine rings are inherently electron-deficient and can be used as platforms for anion-π binding, especially when incorporated into macrocyclic or cage-like structures.\n\n4. Fluorinated aromatic hosts: Perfluorinated benzene or naphthalene derivatives are highly π-acidic and have been shown to bind anions via anion-π interactions, either as simple hosts or as part of larger supramolecular assemblies.\n\n5.
